				<div class="page-heading">
					<h1 id="section_0">Bank of America</h1>
					<div class="tagline"></div>
				</div>

				<div class="minerva__subtitle"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Bank_of_America_Corp&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Bank of America Corp">Bank of America Corp</a>)</span></div>
			</div>
			
			<div id="bodyContent" class="content"><div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="mw-parser-output"><section class="mf-section-0" id="mf-section-0"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about a commercial bank unaffiliated with any government. For the central bank of the United States, see <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Reserve System">Federal Reserve System</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"BofA" redirects here. For the French illustrator, see <a href="/wiki/Gus_Bofa" title="Gus Bofa">Gus Bofa</a>.</div>

<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<p>The <b>Bank of America Corporation</b> (abbreviated as <b>BofA</b>) is an American multinational <a href="/wiki/Investment_banking" title="Investment banking">investment bank</a> and <a href="/wiki/Financial_services" title="Financial services">financial services</a> company headquartered in <a href="/wiki/Charlotte" class="mw-redirect" title="Charlotte">Charlotte</a>, with central hubs in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minneapolis" title="Minneapolis">Minneapolis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a>. Founded in <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a>, Bank of America was formed through NationsBank's acquisition of BankAmerica in 1998. It is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_banks_in_the_United_States" title="List of largest banks in the United States">second largest banking institution</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, after <a href="/wiki/JPMorgan_Chase" title="JPMorgan Chase">JPMorgan Chase</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_financial_services_companies_by_revenue" title="List of largest financial services companies by revenue">ninth largest financial services company</a> in the world (by revenue). As a part of the <a href="/wiki/Big_Four_(banking)#United_States" title="Big Four (banking)">Big Four</a>, it services approximately 10.73% of all American bank deposits, in direct competition with <a href="/wiki/Citigroup" title="Citigroup">Citigroup</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wells_Fargo" title="Wells Fargo">Wells Fargo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/JPMorgan_Chase" title="JPMorgan Chase">JPMorgan Chase</a>. Its primary financial services revolve around <a href="/wiki/Commercial_bank" title="Commercial bank">commercial banking</a>, wealth management, and investment banking.

srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Decrease2.svg/17px-Decrease2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Decrease2.svg/22px-Decrease2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="300"> <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$264.74 billion</span> (2018)<sup id="cite_ref-10K_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10K-1">[1]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;">Owner</th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><a href="/wiki/Berkshire_Hathaway" title="Berkshire Hathaway">Berkshire Hathaway</a> (10%)<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">Number of employees</div></th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;">204,489 (2019)<sup id="cite_ref-10K_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10K-1">[1]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><a href="/wiki/Division_(business)" title="Division (business)">Divisions</a></th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><a href="/wiki/BofA_Securities" title="BofA Securities">BofA Securities</a><br><a href="/wiki/Merrill_(company)" title="Merrill (company)">Merrill</a><br><a href="/wiki/Bank_of_America_Private_Bank" title="Bank of America Private Bank">Bank of America Private Bank</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><a href="/wiki/Basel_III" title="Basel III">Capital ratio</a></th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;">11.8% (2017)<sup id="cite_ref-10K_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10K-1">[1]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;">Rating</th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><a href="/wiki/Moody%27s_Investors_Service" title="Moody's Investors Service">Moody's</a>: Baa1<br><a href="/wiki/Standard_%26_Poor%27s" title="Standard &amp; Poor's">S&amp;P</a>: BBB<br><a href="/wiki/Fitch_Ratings" title="Fitch Ratings">Fitch</a>: A</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;">Website</th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.bankofamerica.com/">bankofamerica.com</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table><p>One branch of its history stretches back to <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Italy_(United_States)" title="Bank of Italy (United States)">Bank of Italy</a>, founded by <a href="/wiki/Amadeo_Giannini" title="Amadeo Giannini">Amadeo Pietro Giannini</a> in 1904, which provided <a href="/wiki/Italian_diaspora" title="Italian diaspora">Italian immigrants</a> who <a href="/wiki/Anti-Italianism" title="Anti-Italianism">faced service discrimination</a> various banking options.<sup id="cite_ref-PBSGiannini_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBSGiannini-3">[3]</a></sup> Originally headquartered in <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco, California</a>, Giannini acquired <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Bank_(Italy)" title="Deutsche Bank (Italy)">Banca d'America e d'Italia</a> (Bank of America and <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>) in 1922. The passage of landmark federal banking legislation facilitated rapid growth in the 1950s, quickly establishing a prominent market share. After suffering a significant loss after the <a href="/wiki/1998_Russian_financial_crisis" title="1998 Russian financial crisis">1998 Russian bond default</a>, BankAmerica, as it was then known, was acquired by the Charlotte-based <a href="/wiki/NationsBank" title="NationsBank">NationsBank</a> for <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>62 billion. Following what was then the <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_mergers_and_acquisitions" title="List of largest mergers and acquisitions">largest bank acquisition in history</a>, the Bank of America Corporation was founded. Through a series of mergers and acquisitions, it built upon its commercial banking business by establishing <a href="/wiki/Merrill_Lynch" class="mw-redirect" title="Merrill Lynch">Merrill Lynch</a> for wealth management and <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_America_Merrill_Lynch" class="mw-redirect" title="Bank of America Merrill Lynch">Bank of America Merrill Lynch</a> for investment banking in 2008 and 2009, respectively (since renamed BofA Securities).<sup id="cite_ref-ATLoffer_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATLoffer-4">[4]</a></sup></p><p>Both Bank of America and Merrill Lynch Wealth Management retain large market shares in their respective offerings. The <a href="/wiki/Investment_banking" title="Investment banking">investment bank</a> is considered within the "<a href="/wiki/Bulge_Bracket" title="Bulge Bracket">Bulge Bracket</a>" as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_investment_banks" title="List of investment banks">third largest investment bank in the world</a>, as of 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bank_of_America&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Its wealth management side manages <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>1.081 trillion in <a href="/wiki/Assets_under_management" title="Assets under management">assets under management</a> (AUM) as the <a href="/wiki/Private_banking#Private_banking_rankings" title="Private banking">second largest wealth manager in the world</a>, after <a href="/wiki/UBS" title="UBS">UBS</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> In commercial banking, Bank of America operates—but does not necessarily maintain retail branches—in all 50 states of the United States, the <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia" class="mw-redirect" title="District of Columbia">District of Columbia</a> and more than 40 other countries.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Its commercial banking footprint encapsulates 46 million consumer and small business relationships at 4,600 banking centers and 15,900 <a href="/wiki/Automated_teller_machine" title="Automated teller machine">automated teller machines</a> (ATMs).
</p><p>The bank's large market share, business activities, and economic impact has led to numerous lawsuits and investigations regarding both mortgages and financial disclosures dating back to the <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%9308" title="Financial crisis of 2007–08">2008 financial crisis</a>. Its corporate practices of servicing the <a href="/wiki/Middle_class" title="Middle class">middle class</a> and wider banking community has yielded a substantial market share since the early 20th century. As of August 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bank_of_America&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Bank of America has a $313.5 billion <a href="/wiki/Market_capitalization" title="Market capitalization">market capitalization</a>, making it the <a href="/wiki/List_of_public_corporations_by_market_capitalization" title="List of public corporations by market capitalization">13th largest company in the world</a>. As the sixth largest American public company, it garnered $102.98 billion in sales as of June 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bank_of_America&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Bank of America was ranked #24 on the 2018 <a href="/wiki/Fortune_500" title="Fortune 500">Fortune 500</a> rankings of the largest United States corporations by total revenue.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Bank of America was named the "World's Best Bank" by the <i><a href="/wiki/Euromoney_Institutional_Investor" title="Euromoney Institutional Investor">Euromoney Institutional Investor</a></i> in their 2018 Awards for Excellence.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none"><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(1)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bank_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History" data-section="1" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-1 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-1">
<p>The Bank of America name first appeared in 1923, with the formation of <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_America,_Los_Angeles" title="Bank of America, Los Angeles">Bank of America, Los Angeles</a>. In 1928, it was acquired by <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Italy_(United_States)" title="Bank of Italy (United States)">Bank of Italy</a> of San Francisco, which took the Bank of America name two years later.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p><p>The eastern portion of the Bank of America franchise can be traced to 1784, when <a href="/wiki/BankBoston#The_Massachusetts_Bank" title="BankBoston">Massachusetts Bank</a> was chartered—the first iteration of <a href="/wiki/FleetBoston_Financial" title="FleetBoston Financial">FleetBoston</a>, which Bank of America acquired in 2004. In 1874, <a href="/wiki/Commercial_National_Bank" title="Commercial National Bank">Commercial National Bank</a> was founded in Charlotte. That bank merged with American Trust Company in 1958 to form American Commercial Bank.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Two years later it became <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_National_Bank" title="North Carolina National Bank">North Carolina National Bank</a> when it merged with Security National Bank of Greensboro. In 1991, it merged with <a href="/wiki/Citizens_%26_Southern_National_Bank" title="Citizens &amp; Southern National Bank">C&amp;S</a>/<a href="/wiki/Sovran_Bank" title="Sovran Bank">Sovran</a> Corporation of <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">Atlanta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norfolk,_Virginia" title="Norfolk, Virginia">Norfolk</a> to form <b><a href="/wiki/NationsBank" title="NationsBank">NationsBank</a>.</b>
</p><p>The central portion of the franchise dates to 1910, when Commercial National Bank and Continental National Bank of Chicago merged in 1910 to form Continental &amp; Commercial National Bank, which evolved into <a href="/wiki/Continental_Illinois" title="Continental Illinois">Continental Illinois National Bank &amp; Trust</a>.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Bank_of_Italy">Bank of Italy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bank_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Bank of Italy" data-section="2" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Italy_(United_States)" title="Bank of Italy (United States)">Bank of Italy (United States)</a></div>
<p>From a naming perspective, the history of Bank of America dates back to October 17, 1904, when <a href="/wiki/Amadeo_Giannini" title="Amadeo Giannini">Amadeo Pietro Giannini</a> founded the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Italy_(United_States)" title="Bank of Italy (United States)">Bank of Italy</a> in San Francisco.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> In 1922, Bank of America, Los Angeles was established with Giannini as a minority investor. The two banks merged in 1928 and consolidated it with other bank holdings to create what would become the largest banking institution in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-14">[14]</a></sup> In 1986, Deutsche Bank AG acquired 100% of Banca d'America e d'Italia, a bank established in Naples in 1917 following the name-change of Banca dell'Italia Meridionale with the latter established in 1918.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In 1918, another corporation, Bancitaly Corporation, was organized by A. P. Giannini, the largest stockholder of which was Stockholders Auxiliary Corporation.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-14">[14]</a></sup> This company acquired the stocks of various banks located in New York City and certain foreign countries.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> In 1918, the Bank opened a Delegation in New York in order to follow American political, economic and financial affairs more closely.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-14">[14]</a></sup> In 1928, Giannini merged his bank with <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_America,_Los_Angeles" title="Bank of America, Los Angeles">Bank of America, Los Angeles</a>, headed by <a href="/wiki/Orra_E._Monnette" title="Orra E. Monnette">Orra E. Monnette</a>. Bank of Italy was renamed on November 3, 1930, to <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_America_NT%26SA" title="Bank of America NT&amp;SA">Bank of America National Trust and Savings Association</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> which was the only such designated bank in the United States at that time. Giannini and Monnette headed the resulting company, serving as co-chairs.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion_in_California">Expansion in California</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bank_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Expansion in California" data-section="3" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Giannini introduced branch banking shortly after 1909 legislation in California allowed for branch banking in the state, establishing the bank's first branch outside San Francisco in 1909 in San Jose. By 1929 the bank had 453 banking offices in California with aggregate resources of over US$1.4 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> There is a replica of the 1909 Bank of Italy branch bank in <a href="/wiki/History_Park_at_Kelley_Park" title="History Park at Kelley Park">History Park</a> in San Jose, and the 1925 <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Italy_Building_(San_Jose,_California)" title="Bank of Italy Building (San Jose, California)">Bank of Italy Building</a> is an important <a href="/wiki/Downtown" title="Downtown">downtown</a> landmark. Giannini sought to build a national bank, expanding into most of the western states as well as into the <a href="/wiki/Insurance_industry" class="mw-redirect" title="Insurance industry">insurance industry</a>, under the aegis of his holding company, <a href="/wiki/Transamerica_Corporation" title="Transamerica Corporation">Transamerica Corporation</a>. In 1953 regulators succeeded in forcing the separation of <a href="/wiki/Transamerica_Corporation" title="Transamerica Corporation">Transamerica Corporation</a> and Bank of America under the <a href="/wiki/Clayton_Antitrust_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Clayton Antitrust Act">Clayton Antitrust Act</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The passage of the <a href="/wiki/Bank_Holding_Company_Act_of_1956" class="mw-redirect" title="Bank Holding Company Act of 1956">Bank Holding Company Act of 1956</a> prohibited banks from owning <a href="/wiki/Non-bank_subsidiary" title="Non-bank subsidiary">non-banking subsidiaries</a> such as insurance companies. Bank of America and Transamerica were separated, with the latter company continuing in the insurance sector. However, federal banking regulators prohibited Bank of America's interstate banking activity, and Bank of America's domestic banks outside California were forced into a separate company that eventually became <a href="/wiki/First_Interstate_Bancorp" title="First Interstate Bancorp">First Interstate Bancorp</a>, later acquired by <a href="/wiki/Wells_Fargo_and_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Wells Fargo and Company">Wells Fargo and Company</a> in 1996. Only in the 1980s, with a change in federal banking legislation and regulation, could Bank of America again expand its domestic consumer banking activity outside California.
</p><p>New technologies also allowed the direct linking of <a href="/wiki/Credit_card" title="Credit card">credit cards</a> with individual bank accounts. In 1958, the bank introduced the BankAmericard, which changed its name to <a href="/wiki/Visa_Inc." title="Visa Inc.">Visa</a> in 1977.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
A consortium of other California banks introduced Master Charge (now <a href="/wiki/MasterCard" class="mw-redirect" title="MasterCard">MasterCard</a>) in 1966 to compete with BankAmericard.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion_outside_California">Expansion outside California</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bank_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Expansion outside California" data-section="4" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Following the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Bank_Holding_Company_Act_of_1956" class="mw-redirect" title="Bank Holding Company Act of 1956">Bank Holding Company Act of 1956</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> BankAmerica Corporation was established<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (March 2020)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> for the purpose of owning and operating Bank of America and its subsidiaries.
</p><p>Bank of America expanded outside <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> in 1983, with its acquisition, orchestrated in part by <a href="/wiki/Stephen_McLin" title="Stephen McLin">Stephen McLin</a>, of <a href="/wiki/Seafirst_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Seafirst Corporation">Seafirst Corporation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">Seattle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a>, and its wholly-owned banking subsidiary, Seattle-First National Bank.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-22">[22]</a></sup> Seafirst was at risk of seizure by the federal government after becoming insolvent due to a series of bad loans to the <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">oil</a> industry. BankAmerica continued to operate its new subsidiary as Seafirst rather than Bank of America until the 1998 merger with NationsBank.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-22">[22]</a></sup></p><p>BankAmerica experienced huge losses in 1986 and 1987 due to the placement of a series of bad loans in the <a href="/wiki/Third_World" title="Third World">Third World</a>, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The company fired its CEO, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Armacost" title="Samuel Armacost"> Sam Armacost</a> in 1986. Though Armacost blamed the problems on his predecessor, <a href="/wiki/Alden_W._Clausen" title="Alden W. Clausen">A.W. (Tom) Clausen</a>, Clausen was appointed to replace Armacost.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The losses resulted in a huge decline of BankAmerica stock, making it vulnerable to a hostile <a href="/wiki/Takeover" title="Takeover">takeover</a>. <a href="/wiki/First_Interstate_Bancorp" title="First Interstate Bancorp">First Interstate Bancorp</a> of Los Angeles (which had originated from banks once owned by BankAmerica), launched such a bid in the fall of 1986, although BankAmerica rebuffed it, mostly by selling operations.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> It sold its FinanceAmerica subsidiary to <a href="/wiki/Chrysler" title="Chrysler">Chrysler</a> and the brokerage firm <a href="/wiki/Charles_Schwab_Corporation" title="Charles Schwab Corporation">Charles Schwab and Co.</a> back to <a href="/wiki/Charles_R._Schwab" title="Charles R. Schwab">Mr. Schwab</a>. It also sold <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_America_and_Italy" class="mw-redirect" title="Bank of America and Italy">Bank of America and Italy</a> to <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Bank" title="Deutsche Bank">Deutsche Bank</a>. By the time of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Monday_(1987)" title="Black Monday (1987)">1987 stock-market crash</a>, BankAmerica's share price had fallen to $8, but by 1992 it had rebounded mightily to become one of the biggest gainers of that half-decade.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p><p>BankAmerica's next big acquisition came in 1992. The company acquired Security Pacific Corporation and its subsidiary <a href="/wiki/Security_Pacific_Bank" title="Security Pacific Bank"> Security Pacific National Bank</a> in California and other banks in <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>, <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)"> Washington</a>, which Security Pacific had acquired in a series of acquisitions in the late 1980s. This represented, at the time, the largest bank acquisition in history.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Federal regulators, however, forced the sale of roughly half of Security Pacific's Washington subsidiary, the former <a href="/wiki/Rainier_Bank" class="mw-redirect" title="Rainier Bank">Rainier Bank</a>, as the combination of Seafirst and Security Pacific Washington would have given BankAmerica too large a share of the market in that state. The Washington branches were divided and sold to West One Bancorp (now <a href="/wiki/U.S._Bancorp" title="U.S. Bancorp">U.S. Bancorp</a>) and <a href="/wiki/KeyBank" title="KeyBank">KeyBank</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Later that year, BankAmerica expanded into Nevada by acquiring Valley Bank of Nevada.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p><p>In 1994 BankAmerica acquired the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Illinois_National_Bank_and_Trust_Co." class="mw-redirect" title="Continental Illinois National Bank and Trust Co.">Continental Illinois National Bank and Trust Co.</a> of Chicago. At the time, no bank possessed the resources to bail out Continental, so the federal government operated the bank for nearly a decade.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a> then regulated branch banking extremely heavily, so Bank of America Illinois was a single-unit bank until the 21st century. BankAmerica moved its national lending department to Chicago in an effort to establish a financial beachhead in the region.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bank_of_America,_Loop_410,_San_Antonio,_TX_IMG_7852.JPG" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Bank_of_America%2C_Loop_410%2C_San_Antonio%2C_TX_IMG_7852.JPG/220px-Bank_of_America%2C_Loop_410%2C_San_Antonio%2C_TX_IMG_7852.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="3456"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 165px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Bank_of_America%2C_Loop_410%2C_San_Antonio%2C_TX_IMG_7852.JPG/220px-Bank_of_America%2C_Loop_410%2C_San_Antonio%2C_TX_IMG_7852.JPG" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="165" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bank_of_America,_Loop_410,_San_Antonio,_TX_IMG_7852.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pyramid-shaped former Bank of America branch building towers over <a href="/wiki/Interstate_410" title="Interstate 410">Interstate 410</a> in <a href="/wiki/San_Antonio,_Texas" class="mw-redirect" title="San Antonio, Texas">San Antonio, Texas</a></div></div></div>
<p>These mergers helped BankAmerica Corporation to once again become the largest U.S. bank holding-company in terms of deposits, but the company fell to second place in 1997 behind North Carolina's fast-growing <a href="/wiki/NationsBank_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="NationsBank Corporation">NationsBank Corporation</a>, and to third in 1998 behind <a href="/wiki/First_Union" title="First Union">First Union</a> Corp.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bank_of_America_logo.svg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Bank_of_America_logo.svg/220px-Bank_of_America_logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="22" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="73"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 22px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Bank_of_America_logo.svg/220px-Bank_of_America_logo.svg.png" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="22" data-srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Bank_of_America_logo.svg/330px-Bank_of_America_logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Bank_of_America_logo.svg/440px-Bank_of_America_logo.svg.png 2x" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bank_of_America_logo.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bank of America logo used since 2018, an updated version of the logo from 1998 to 2018</div></div></div>
<p>On the capital markets side, the acquisition of Continental Illinois helped BankAmerica to build a leveraged finance origination- and distribution-business, which allowed the firm's existing broker-dealer, BancAmerica Securities (originally named BA Securities), to become a full-service franchise.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> In addition, in 1997, BankAmerica acquired <a href="/wiki/Robertson_Stephens" title="Robertson Stephens">Robertson Stephens</a>, a San Francisco–based investment bank specializing in high technology for $540 million.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Robertson Stephens was integrated into BancAmerica Securities and the combined subsidiary was renamed "BancAmerica Robertson Stephens".<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Merger_of_NationsBank_and_BankAmerica">Merger of NationsBank and BankAmerica</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bank_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Merger of NationsBank and BankAmerica" data-section="5" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BankAmerica_logo.png" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/BankAmerica_logo.png/220px-BankAmerica_logo.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="47" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="358" data-file-height="76"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 47px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/BankAmerica_logo.png/220px-BankAmerica_logo.png" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="47" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BankAmerica_logo.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Logo of the former Bank of America (BA), 1969–1998</div></div></div>
<p>In 1997, BankAmerica lent <a href="/wiki/Hedge_fund" title="Hedge fund">hedge fund</a> <a href="/wiki/D._E._Shaw_%26_Co." title="D. E. Shaw &amp; Co.">D. E. Shaw &amp; Co.</a> $1.4 billion in order to run various businesses for the bank.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> However, D.E. Shaw suffered significant loss after the <a href="/wiki/1998_Russian_financial_crisis" title="1998 Russian financial crisis">1998 Russia bond default</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/NationsBank" title="NationsBank">NationsBank</a> of Charlotte acquired BankAmerica in October 1998 in what was the largest bank acquisition in history at that time.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p><p>While NationsBank was the nominal survivor, the merged bank took the better-known name of Bank of America. Hence, the holding company was renamed Bank of America Corporation, while NationsBank, N.A. merged with Bank of America NT&amp;SA to form Bank of America, N.A. as the remaining legal bank entity.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The combined bank operates under Federal Charter 13044, which was granted to Giannini's Bank of Italy on March 1, 1927. However, the merged company was and still is headquartered in Charlotte, and retains NationsBank's pre-1998 stock price history. All <a href="/wiki/U.S._Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" title="U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission">U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission</a> (SEC) filings before 1998 are listed under NationsBank, not Bank of America. NationsBank president, chairman and CEO <a href="/wiki/Hugh_McColl" title="Hugh McColl">Hugh McColl</a>, took on the same roles with the merged company.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p><p>In 1998, Bank of America possessed combined assets of $570 billion, as well as 4,800 branches in 22 states.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Despite the size of the two companies, federal regulators insisted only upon the divestiture of 13 branches in <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>, in towns that would be left with only a single bank following the combination.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The broker-dealer, NationsBanc Montgomery Securities, was named <a href="/wiki/Banc_of_America_Securities" class="mw-redirect" title="Banc of America Securities">Banc of America Securities</a> in 1998.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="2001_to_present">2001 to present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bank_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 2001 to present" data-section="6" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Porter_Ranch_Bank_of_America.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Porter_Ranch_Bank_of_America.jpg/220px-Porter_Ranch_Bank_of_America.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="621"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 137px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Porter_Ranch_Bank_of_America.jpg/220px-Porter_Ranch_Bank_of_America.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="137" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Porter_Ranch_Bank_of_America.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Typical Bank of America local office in Los Angeles</div></div></div>
<p>In 2001, McColl stepped down and named <a href="/wiki/Ken_Lewis_(executive)" title="Ken Lewis (executive)">Ken Lewis</a> as his successor.
</p><p>In 2004, Bank of America announced it would purchase Boston-based bank <a href="/wiki/FleetBoston_Financial" title="FleetBoston Financial">FleetBoston Financial</a> for $47 billion in cash and stock.<sup id="cite_ref-fleetboston-merger_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fleetboston-merger-38">[38]</a></sup> By merging with Bank of America, all of its banks and branches were given the Bank of America logo. At the time of merger, FleetBoston was the seventh largest bank in United States with $197 billion in assets, over 20 million customers and revenue of $12 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-fleetboston-merger_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fleetboston-merger-38">[38]</a></sup> Hundreds of FleetBoston workers lost their jobs or were demoted, according to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Boston_Globe" title="The Boston Globe">The Boston Globe</a></i>.
</p><p>On June 30, 2005, Bank of America announced it would purchase credit card giant <a href="/wiki/MBNA" title="MBNA">MBNA</a> for $35 billion in cash and stock. The <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve_Board" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Reserve Board">Federal Reserve Board</a> gave final approval to the merger on December 15, 2005, and the merger closed on January 1, 2006. The acquisition of MBNA provided Bank of America a leading domestic and foreign credit card issuer. The combined Bank of America Card Services organization, including the former MBNA, had more than 40 million U.S. accounts and nearly $140 billion in outstanding balances. Under Bank of America the operation was renamed FIA Card Services.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bank_of_America_footprint.png" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Bank_of_America_footprint.png/220px-Bank_of_America_footprint.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="115" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="6000" data-file-height="3126"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 115px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Bank_of_America_footprint.png/220px-Bank_of_America_footprint.png" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="115" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bank_of_America_footprint.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bank of America footprint</div></div></div>
<p>Bank of America operated under the name BankBoston in many other Latin American countries, including Brazil. In 2006, Bank of America sold BankBoston's operations to Brazilian bank Banco Itaú, in exchange for Itaú shares. The BankBoston name and trademarks were not part of the transaction and, as part of the sale agreement, cannot be used by Bank of America (ending the BankBoston brand).
</p><p>In May 2006, Bank of America and <a href="/wiki/Banco_Ita%C3%BA" title="Banco Itaú">Banco Itaú</a> (Investimentos Itaú S.A.) entered into an acquisition agreement through which Itaú agreed to acquire BankBoston's operations in Brazil and was granted an exclusive right to purchase Bank of America's operations in <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uruguay" title="Uruguay">Uruguay</a>. The deal was signed in August 2006 under which Itaú agreed to purchase Bank of America's operations in Chile and Uruguay. Prior to the transaction, BankBoston's Brazilian operations included asset management, private banking, a credit card portfolio, and small, middle-market, and large corporate segments. It had 66 branches and 203,000 clients in Brazil. BankBoston in Chile had 44 branches and 58,000 clients and in Uruguay it had 15 branches. In addition, there was a credit card company, OCA, in Uruguay, which had 23 branches. BankBoston N.A. in Uruguay, together with OCA, jointly served 372,000 clients. While the BankBoston name and trademarks were not part of the transaction, as part of the sale agreement, they cannot be used by Bank of America in Brazil, Chile or Uruguay following the transactions. Hence, the BankBoston name has disappeared from Brazil, Chile and Uruguay. The Itaú stock received by Bank of America in the transactions has allowed Bank of America's stake in Itaú to reach 11.51%. Banco de Boston de Brazil had been founded in 1947.
</p><p>On November 20, 2006, Bank of America announced the purchase of <a href="/wiki/The_United_States_Trust_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="The United States Trust Company">The United States Trust Company</a> for $3.3 billion, from the <a href="/wiki/Charles_Schwab_Corporation" title="Charles Schwab Corporation">Charles Schwab Corporation</a>. US Trust had about $100 billion of <a href="/wiki/Assets_under_management" title="Assets under management">assets under management</a> and over 150 years of experience. The deal closed July 1, 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p><p>On September 14, 2007, Bank of America won approval from the Federal Reserve to acquire <a href="/wiki/LaSalle_Bank_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="LaSalle Bank Corporation">LaSalle Bank Corporation</a> from <a href="/wiki/ABN_AMRO" title="ABN AMRO">ABN AMRO</a> for $21 billion. With this purchase, Bank of America possessed $1.7 trillion in assets. A Dutch court blocked the sale until it was later approved in July. The acquisition was completed on October 1, 2007. Many of LaSalle's branches and offices had already taken over smaller regional banks within the previous decade, such as Lansing and Detroit based <a href="/wiki/Michigan_National_Bank" title="Michigan National Bank">Michigan National Bank</a>. The acquisition also included the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Marathon" title="Chicago Marathon">Chicago Marathon</a> event, which ABN AMRO acquired in 1996. Bank of America took over the event starting with the 2007 race.
</p><p>The deal increased Bank of America's presence in <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a> by 411 branches, 17,000 commercial bank clients, 1.4 million retail customers, and 1,500 ATMs. Bank of America became the largest bank in the Chicago market with 197 offices and 14% of the deposit share, surpassing <a href="/wiki/JPMorgan_Chase" title="JPMorgan Chase">JPMorgan Chase</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/LaSalle_Bank" title="LaSalle Bank">LaSalle Bank</a> and <a href="/wiki/LaSalle_Bank_Midwest" class="mw-redirect" title="LaSalle Bank Midwest">LaSalle Bank Midwest</a> branches adopted the Bank of America name on May 5, 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p><p>Ken Lewis, who had lost the title of Chairman of the Board, announced that he would retire as CEO effective December 31, 2009, in part due to controversy and legal investigations concerning the purchase of Merrill Lynch. <a href="/wiki/Brian_Moynihan" title="Brian Moynihan">Brian Moynihan</a> became President and CEO effective January 1, 2010, and afterward credit card charge offs and delinquencies declined in January. Bank of America also repaid the $45 billion it had received from the Troubled Assets Relief Program.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Acquisition_of_Countrywide_Financial">Acquisition of Countrywide Financial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bank_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Acquisition of Countrywide Financial" data-section="7" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>On August 23, 2007, the company announced a $2 billion <a href="/wiki/Repurchase_agreement" title="Repurchase agreement">repurchase agreement</a> for <a href="/wiki/Countrywide_Financial" class="mw-redirect" title="Countrywide Financial">Countrywide Financial</a>. This purchase of <a href="/wiki/Preferred_stock" title="Preferred stock">preferred stock</a> was arranged to provide a <a href="/wiki/Return_on_investment" title="Return on investment">return on investment</a> of 7.25% <i><a href="/wiki/Per_annum" title="Per annum">per annum</a></i> and provided the option to purchase <a href="/wiki/Common_stock" title="Common stock">common stock</a> at a price of $18 per share.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p><p>On January 11, 2008, Bank of America announced that it would buy Countrywide Financial for $4.1 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> In March 2008, it was reported that the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">Federal Bureau of Investigation</a> (FBI) was investigating Countrywide for possible fraud relating to home loans and mortgages.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> This news did not hinder the acquisition, which was completed in July 2008,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> giving the bank a substantial market share of the mortgage business, and access to Countrywide's resources for servicing mortgages.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The acquisition was seen as preventing a potential bankruptcy for Countrywide. Countrywide, however, denied that it was close to bankruptcy. Countrywide provided mortgage servicing for nine million mortgages valued at $1.4 trillion as of December 31, 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p><p>This purchase made Bank of America Corporation the leading mortgage originator and servicer in the U.S., controlling 20–25% of the home loan market.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The deal was structured to merge Countrywide with the Red Oak Merger Corporation, which Bank of America created as an independent subsidiary. It has been suggested that the deal was structured this way to prevent a potential bankruptcy stemming from large losses in Countrywide hurting the parent organization by keeping Countrywide <a href="/wiki/Bankruptcy_remote" title="Bankruptcy remote">bankruptcy remote</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Countrywide Financial has changed its name to <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_America_Home_Loans" title="Bank of America Home Loans">Bank of America Home Loans</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BOAChart.svg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/BOAChart.svg/220px-BOAChart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="123" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="831" data-file-height="464"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 123px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/BOAChart.svg/220px-BOAChart.svg.png" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="123" data-srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/BOAChart.svg/330px-BOAChart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/BOAChart.svg/440px-BOAChart.svg.png 2x" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BOAChart.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chart showing the trajectory of BOA share value and transaction volume during the 2007–2009 Financial Crisis</div></div></div>
<p><span id="Discrimination"></span>
In December 2011, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">Justice Department</a> announced a $335 million <a href="/wiki/Settlement_agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Settlement agreement">settlement</a> with Bank of America over discriminatory lending practice at Countrywide Financial. <a href="/wiki/United_States_Attorney_General" title="United States Attorney General">Attorney General</a> <a href="/wiki/Eric_Holder" title="Eric Holder">Eric Holder</a> said a federal probe found <a href="/wiki/Mortgage_discrimination" title="Mortgage discrimination">discrimination</a> against qualified African-American and Latino borrowers from 2004 to 2008. He said that minority borrowers who qualified for <a href="/wiki/Prime_lending_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Prime lending rate">prime loans</a> were steered into higher-interest-rate <a href="/wiki/Subprime_loans" class="mw-redirect" title="Subprime loans">subprime loans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Acquisition_of_Merrill_Lynch">Acquisition of Merrill Lynch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bank_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Acquisition of Merrill Lynch" data-section="8" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>On September 14, 2008, Bank of America announced its intention to purchase <a href="/wiki/Merrill_Lynch" class="mw-redirect" title="Merrill Lynch">Merrill Lynch &amp; Co., Inc.</a> in an all-stock deal worth approximately $50 billion. Merrill Lynch was at the time within days of collapse, and the acquisition effectively saved Merrill from bankruptcy.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Around the same time Bank of America was reportedly also in talks to purchase <a href="/wiki/Lehman_Brothers" title="Lehman Brothers">Lehman Brothers</a>, however a lack of government guarantees caused the bank to abandon talks with Lehman.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Lehman Brothers filed for bankruptcy the same day Bank of America announced its plans to acquire Merrill Lynch.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> This acquisition made Bank of America the largest <a href="/wiki/Financial_services" title="Financial services">financial services</a> company in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Temasek_Holdings" title="Temasek Holdings">Temasek Holdings</a>, the largest shareholder of Merrill Lynch &amp; Co., Inc., briefly became one of the largest shareholders of Bank of America, with a 3% stake.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> However, taking a loss <a href="/wiki/Reuters" title="Reuters">Reuters</a> estimated at $3 billion, the <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_wealth_fund" title="Sovereign wealth fund">sovereign wealth fund</a> sold its whole stake in Bank of America in the first quarter of 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p><p>Shareholders of both companies approved the acquisition on December 5, 2008, and the deal closed January 1, 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Bank of America had planned to retain various members of the then Merrill Lynch's CEO, <a href="/wiki/John_Thain" title="John Thain">John Thain</a>'s management team after the merger.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> However, after Thain was removed from his position, most of his allies left. The departure of <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Chai" title="Nelson Chai">Nelson Chai</a>, who had been named Asia-Pacific president, left just one of Thain's hires in place: Tom Montag, head of sales and trading.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p><p>The bank, in its January 16, 2009, earnings release, revealed massive losses at Merrill Lynch in the fourth quarter, which necessitated an infusion of money that had previously been negotiated<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> with the government as part of the government-persuaded deal for the bank to acquire Merrill. Merrill recorded an operating loss of $21.5 billion in the quarter, mainly in its sales and trading operations, led by Tom Montag. The bank also disclosed it tried to abandon the deal in December after the extent of Merrill's trading losses surfaced, but was compelled to complete the merger by the U.S. government. The bank's stock price sank to $7.18, its lowest level in 17 years, after announcing earnings and the Merrill mishap. The market capitalization of Bank of America, including Merrill Lynch, was then $45 billion, less than the $50 billion it offered for Merrill just four months earlier, and down $108 billion from the merger announcement.
</p><p>Bank of America CEO Kenneth Lewis testified before Congress<sup id="cite_ref-ATLoffer_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATLoffer-4">[4]</a></sup> that he had some misgivings about the acquisition of Merrill Lynch, and that federal officials pressured him to proceed with the deal or face losing his job and endangering the bank's relationship with federal regulators.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p><p>Lewis' statement is backed up by internal emails subpoenaed by Republican lawmakers on the House Oversight Committee.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> In one of the emails, Richmond Federal Reserve President <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_M._Lacker" title="Jeffrey M. Lacker">Jeffrey Lacker</a> threatened that if the acquisition did not go through, and later Bank of America were forced to request federal assistance, the management of Bank of America would be "gone". Other emails, read by Congressman <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Kucinich" title="Dennis Kucinich">Dennis Kucinich</a> during the course of Lewis' testimony, state that Mr. Lewis had foreseen the outrage from his shareholders that the purchase of Merrill would cause, and asked government regulators to issue a letter stating that the government had ordered him to complete the deal to acquire Merrill. Lewis, for his part, states he didn't recall requesting such a letter.
</p><p>The acquisition made Bank of America the number one <a href="/wiki/Securities_underwriting" class="mw-redirect" title="Securities underwriting">underwriter</a> of global <a href="/wiki/High-yield_debt" title="High-yield debt">high-yield debt</a>, the third largest underwriter of global equity and the ninth largest adviser on global mergers and acquisitions.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> As the credit crisis eased, losses at Merrill Lynch subsided, and the subsidiary generated $3.7 billion of Bank of America's $4.2 billion in profit by the end of quarter one in 2009, and over 25% in quarter 3 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBergMLprofits_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBergMLprofits-66">[66]</a></sup></p><p>On September 28, 2012, Bank of America settled the class action lawsuit over the Merrill Lynch acquisition and will pay $2.43 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> This was one of the first major securities class action lawsuits stemming from the financial crisis of 2007–2008 to settle. Many major financial institutions had a stake in this lawsuit, including <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Clearing_Corporation" title="Chicago Clearing Corporation">Chicago Clearing Corporation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hedge_fund" title="Hedge fund">hedge funds</a>, and bank trusts, due to the belief that Bank of America stock was a sure investment.
</p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Federal_Troubled_Asset_Relief_Program">Federal Troubled Asset Relief Program</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bank_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Federal Troubled Asset Relief Program" data-section="9" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>Bank of America received $20 billion in the federal bailout from the U.S. government through the <a href="/wiki/Troubled_Asset_Relief_Program" title="Troubled Asset Relief Program">Troubled Asset Relief Program</a> (TARP) on January 16, 2009, and a guarantee of $118 billion in potential losses at the company.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> This was in addition to the $25 billion given to them in the fall of 2008 through TARP. The additional payment was part of a deal with the U.S. government to preserve Bank of America's merger with the troubled investment firm Merrill Lynch.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Since then, members of the U.S. Congress have expressed considerable concern about how this money has been spent, especially since some of the recipients have been accused of misusing the bailout money.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Then CEO <a href="/wiki/Ken_Lewis_(executive)" title="Ken Lewis (executive)">Ken Lewis</a> was quoted as claiming "We are still lending, and we are lending far more because of the TARP program." Members of the U.S. House of Representatives, however, were skeptical and quoted many anecdotes about loan applicants (particularly small business owners) being denied loans and credit card holders facing stiffer terms on the debt in their card accounts.
</p><p>According to an article in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> published on March 15, 2009, Bank of America received an additional $5.2 billion in government bailout money, channeled through <a href="/wiki/American_International_Group" title="American International Group">American International Group</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p><p>As a result of its federal bailout and management problems, <i>The Wall Street Journal</i> reported that the Bank of America was operating under a secret "memorandum of understanding" (MOU) from the U.S. government that requires it to "overhaul its board and address perceived problems with risk and liquidity management". With the federal action, the institution has taken several steps, including arranging for six of its <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_America#Board_of_directors" title="Bank of America">directors</a> to resign and forming a Regulatory Impact Office. Bank of America faces several deadlines in July and August and if not met, could face harsher penalties by federal regulators. Bank of America did not respond to <i>The Wall Street Journal</i> story.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p><p>On December 2, 2009, Bank of America announced it would repay the entire $45 billion it received in TARP and exit the program, using $26.2 billion of excess liquidity along with $18.6 billion to be gained in "common equivalent securities" (<a href="/wiki/Tier_1_capital" title="Tier 1 capital">Tier 1 capital</a>). The bank announced it had completed the repayment on December 9. Bank of America's <a href="/wiki/Ken_Lewis_(executive)" title="Ken Lewis (executive)">Ken Lewis</a> said during the announcement, "We appreciate the critical role that the U.S. government played last fall in helping to stabilize financial markets, and we are pleased to be able to fully repay the investment, with interest.... As America's largest bank, we have a responsibility to make good on the taxpayers' investment, and our record shows that we have been able to fulfill that commitment while continuing to lend."<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AFP_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AFP-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Bonus_settlement">Bonus settlement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bank_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Bonus settlement" data-section="10" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>On August 3, 2009, Bank of America agreed to pay a $33 million fine, without admission or denial of charges, to the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" title="U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission">U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission</a> (SEC) over the non-disclosure of an agreement to pay up to $5.8 billion of bonuses at Merrill. The bank approved the bonuses before the merger but did not disclose them to its shareholders when the shareholders were considering approving the Merrill acquisition, in December 2008. The issue was originally investigated by <a href="/wiki/New_York_Attorney_General" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Attorney General">New York Attorney General</a> <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Cuomo" title="Andrew Cuomo">Andrew Cuomo</a>, who commented after the suit and announced settlement that "the timing of the bonuses, as well as the disclosures relating to them, constituted a 'surprising fit of corporate irresponsibility<span style="padding-right:.15em;">'</span>" and "our investigation of these and other matters pursuant to New York's <a href="/wiki/Martin_Act" title="Martin Act">Martin Act</a> will continue." Congressman Kucinich commented at the same time that "This may not be the last fine that Bank of America pays for how it handled its merger of Merrill Lynch."<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> A federal judge, <a href="/wiki/Jed_Rakoff" class="mw-redirect" title="Jed Rakoff">Jed Rakoff</a>, in an unusual action, refused to approve the settlement on August 5.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
A first hearing before the judge on August 10 was at times heated, and he was "sharply critic[al]" of the bonuses. David Rosenfeld represented the SEC, and Lewis J. Liman, son of <a href="/wiki/Arthur_L._Liman" title="Arthur L. Liman">Arthur L. Liman</a>, represented the bank. The actual amount of bonuses paid was $3.6 billion, of which $850 million was "guaranteed" and the rest was shared amongst 39,000 workers who received average payments of $91,000; 696 people received more than $1 million in bonuses; at least one person received a more than $33 million bonus.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p><p>On September 14, the judge rejected the settlement and told the parties to prepare for trial to begin no later than February 1, 2010. The judge focused much of his criticism on the fact that the fine in the case would be paid by the bank's shareholders, who were the ones that were supposed to have been injured by the lack of disclosure. He wrote, "It is quite something else for the very management that is accused of having lied to its shareholders to determine how much of those victims' money should be used to make the case against the management go away," ... "The proposed settlement," the judge continued, "suggests a rather cynical relationship between the parties: the S.E.C. gets to claim that it is exposing wrongdoing on the part of the Bank of America in a high-profile merger; the bank's management gets to claim that they have been coerced into an onerous settlement by overzealous regulators. And all this is done at the expense, not only of the shareholders, but also of the truth."<sup id="cite_ref-NYT10_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT10-78">[78]</a></sup></p><p>While ultimately deferring to the SEC, in February 2010, Judge Rakoff approved a revised settlement with a $150 million fine "reluctantly", calling the accord "half-baked justice at best" and "inadequate and misguided". Addressing one of the concerns he raised in September, the fine will be "distributed only to Bank of America shareholders harmed by the non-disclosures, or 'legacy shareholders', an improvement on the prior $33 million while still "paltry", according to the judge. Case: SEC v. Bank of America Corp., 09-cv-06829, <a href="/wiki/United_States_District_Court_for_the_Southern_District_of_New_York" title="United States District Court for the Southern District of New York">United States District Court for the Southern District of New York</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p><p>Investigations also were held on this issue in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Oversight_and_Government_Reform" class="mw-redirect" title="United States House Committee on Oversight and Government Reform">United States House Committee on Oversight and Government Reform</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-NYT10_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT10-78">[78]</a></sup> under chairman <a href="/wiki/Edolphus_Towns" title="Edolphus Towns">Edolphus Towns</a> (D-NY)<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> and in its investigative <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Oversight_and_Government_Reform_Subcommittee_on_Domestic_Policy" title="United States House Oversight and Government Reform Subcommittee on Domestic Policy">Domestic Policy Subcommittee</a> under Kucinich.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Fraud">Fraud</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bank_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Fraud" data-section="11" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>In 2010, the U.S. government accused the bank of defrauding schools, hospitals, and dozens of state and local government organizations via misconduct and illegal activities involving the investment of proceeds from municipal bond sales. As a result, the bank agreed to pay $137.7 million, including $25 million to the Internal Revenue service and $4.5 million to state attorney general, to the affected organizations to settle the allegations.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p><p>Former bank official Douglas Campbell pleaded guilty to antitrust, conspiracy and wire fraud charges. As of January 2011<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bank_of_America&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, other bankers and brokers are under indictment or investigation.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p><p>On October 24, 2012, the top <a href="/wiki/Federal_prosecutor" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal prosecutor">federal prosecutor</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a> filed a <a href="/wiki/Lawsuit" title="Lawsuit">lawsuit</a> alleging that Bank of America fraudulently cost American taxpayers more than $1 billion when Countrywide Financial sold toxic mortgages to <a href="/wiki/Fannie_Mae" title="Fannie Mae">Fannie Mae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Freddie_Mac" title="Freddie Mac">Freddie Mac</a>. The scheme was called 'Hustle', or High Speed Swim Lane.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nytimes_hustle_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes_hustle-85">[85]</a></sup> On May 23, 2016 the Second U.S. Circuit Court of Appeals ruled that the finding of fact by the jury that low quality mortgages were supplied by Countrywide to Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac in the "Hustle" case supported only "intentional breach of contract," not fraud. The action, for civil fraud, relied on provisions of the <a href="/wiki/Financial_Institutions_Reform,_Recovery,_and_Enforcement_Act_of_1989#Use_with_respect_to_the_subprime_mortgage_crisis" title="Financial Institutions Reform, Recovery, and Enforcement Act of 1989">Financial Institutions Reform, Recovery and Enforcement Act</a>. The decision turned on lack of intent to defraud at the time the contract to supply mortgages was made.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ52316_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ52316-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span id="Downsizing_.282011_to_2014.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Downsizing_(2011_to_2014)">Downsizing (2011 to 2014)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bank_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Downsizing (2011 to 2014)" data-section="12" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>During 2011, Bank of America began conducting personnel reductions of an estimated 36,000 people, contributing to intended savings of $5 billion per year by 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p><p>In December 2011, <i>Forbes</i> ranked Bank of America's financial wealth 91st out of the nation's largest 100 banks and thrift institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p><p>Bank of America cut around 16,000 jobs in a quicker fashion by the end of 2012 as revenue continued to decline because of new regulations and a slow economy. This put a plan one year ahead of time to eliminate 30,000 jobs under a cost-cutting program, called Project New BAC.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
In the first quarter of 2014, <a href="/wiki/Berkshire_Hathaway" title="Berkshire Hathaway">Berkshire</a> bank purchased 20 Bank of America branches in Central and eastern New York for 14.4 million dollars. The branches were from Utica/Rome region and down the Mohawk Valley east to the capital region.
</p><p>In April and May 2014, Bank of America sold two dozen branches in Michigan to <a href="/wiki/Huntington_Bancshares" title="Huntington Bancshares">Huntington Bancshares</a>. The locations were converted to Huntington National Bank branches in September.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p><p>As part of its new strategy Bank of America is focused on growing its mobile banking platform. As of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bank_of_America&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Bank of America has 31 million active online users and 16 million mobile users. Its retail banking branches have decreased to 4,900 as a result of increased mobile banking use and a decline in customer branch visits. By 2018, the number of mobile users has increased to 25.3 million and the number of locations fell to 4,411 at the end of June.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Sale_of_stake_in_China_Construction_Bank">Sale of stake in China Construction Bank</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bank_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Sale of stake in China Construction Bank" data-section="13" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>In 2005, Bank of America acquired a 9% stake in <a href="/wiki/China_Construction_Bank" title="China Construction Bank">China Construction Bank</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Big_Four_(banking)#China" title="Big Four (banking)">Big Four banks in China</a>, for $3 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> It represented the company's largest foray into China's growing banking sector. Bank of America has offices in Hong Kong, Shanghai, and <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a> and was looking to greatly expand its Chinese business as a result of this deal. In 2008, Bank of America was awarded Project Finance Deal of the Year at the 2008 ALB Hong Kong Law Awards.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> In November 2011, Bank of America announced plans to divest most of its stake in the China Construction Bank.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p><p>In September 2013, Bank of America sold its remaining stake in the <a href="/wiki/China_Construction_Bank" title="China Construction Bank">China Construction Bank</a> for as much as $1.5 billion, marking the firm's full exit from the country.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span id=".2417_billion_settlement_with_Justice_Department"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="$17_billion_settlement_with_Justice_Department">$17 billion settlement with Justice Department</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bank_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: $17 billion settlement with Justice Department" data-section="14" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>In August 2014, Bank of America agreed to a near–$17 billion deal to settle claims against it relating to the sale of toxic mortgage-linked securities including subprime home loans, in what was believed to be the largest settlement in U.S. corporate history. The bank agreed with the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">U.S. Justice Department</a> to pay $9.65 billion in fines, and $7 billion in relief to the victims of the faulty loans which included homeowners, borrowers, pension funds and municipalities.<sup id="cite_ref-BAsettlement_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BAsettlement-96">[96]</a></sup> Real estate economist Jed Kolko said the settlement is a "drop in the bucket" compared to the $700 billion in damages done to 11 million homeowners. Since the settlement covered such a substantial portion of the market, he said for most consumers "you're out of luck."<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p><p>Much of the government's prosecution was based on information provided by three whistleblowers – Shareef Abdou (a senior vice president at the bank), Robert Madsen (a professional appraiser employed by a bank subsidiary) and Edward O'Donnell (a Fannie Mae official). The three men received $170 million in whistleblower awards.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="DOD_Community_Bank">DOD Community Bank</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bank_of_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: DOD Community Bank" data-section="15" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DOD_Community_Bank_operated_by_Bank_of_America_logo.png" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/46/DOD_Community_Bank_operated_by_Bank_of_America_logo.png" decoding="async" width="180" height="40" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="180" data-file-height="40"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 180px;height: 40px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/46/DOD_Community_Bank_operated_by_Bank_of_America_logo.png" data-alt="" data-width="180" data-height="40" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DOD_Community_Bank_operated_by_Bank_of_America_logo.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>DOD Community Bank logo</div></div></div>
